<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects">Effects</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:War_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/War_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/220px-War_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png" width="220" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/War_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/330px-War_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/War_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/440px-War_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="415" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:War_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Disability-adjusted_life_year" title="Disability-adjusted life year">Disability-adjusted life year</a> for war per 100,000&#160;inhabitants in 2004<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
<div class="refbegin columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #b3b3b3; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;no data</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffff65; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;less than 100</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fff200; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;100–200</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffdc00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;200–600</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffc600; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;600–1000</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ffb000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1000–1400</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff9a00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1400–1800</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff8400; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;1800–2200</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff6e00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;2200–2600</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff5800; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;2600–3000</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff4200; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;3000–8000</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ff2c00; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;8000–8800</div>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #cb0000; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">&#160;</span>&#160;more than 8800</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_military_personnel">On military personnel</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Military_personnel" title="Military personnel">Military personnel</a> subject to combat in war often suffer mental and physical injuries, including depression, <a href="/wiki/Posttraumatic_stress_disorder" title="Posttraumatic stress disorder">posttraumatic stress disorder</a>, disease, injury, and death.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In every war in which American soldiers have fought in, the chances of becoming a psychiatric casualty – of being debilitated for some period of time as a consequence of the stresses of military life – were greater than the chances of being killed by enemy fire.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <i>No More Heroes</i>, Richard Gabriel<sup id="cite_ref-War_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-War-31">[31]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<p>During World War II, research conducted by <a href="/wiki/US_Army" class="mw-redirect" title="US Army">US Army</a> <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_general_(United_States)" title="Brigadier general (United States)">Brigadier General</a> <a href="/wiki/S.L.A._Marshall" title="S.L.A. Marshall">S.L.A. Marshall</a> found, on average, 15% to 20% of American riflemen in WWII combat fired at the enemy.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1996_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1996-51">[51]</a></sup> In Civil War Collector’s Encyclopedia, F.A. Lord notes that of the 27,574 discarded muskets found on the Gettysburg battlefield, nearly 90% were loaded, with 12,000 loaded more than once and 6,000 loaded 3 to 10 times. These studies suggest most military personnel resist firing their weapons in combat, that – as some theorists argue – human beings have an inherent resistance to killing their fellow human beings.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1996_51-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1996-51">[51]</a></sup> Swank and Marchand’s WWII study found that after sixty days of continuous combat, 98% of all surviving military personnel will become psychiatric casualties. Psychiatric casualties manifest themselves in fatigue cases, confusional states, conversion hysteria, anxiety, obsessional and compulsive states, and character disorders.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1996_51-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1996-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>One-tenth of mobilised American men were hospitalised for mental disturbances between 1942 and 1945, and after thirty-five days of uninterrupted combat, 98% of them manifested psychiatric disturbances in varying degrees.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <i>14–18: Understanding the Great War</i>, Stéphane Audoin-Rouzeau, Annette Becker<sup id="cite_ref-War_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-War-31">[31]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Apotheosis.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Apotheosis.jpg/200px-Apotheosis.jpg" width="200" height="127" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Apotheosis.jpg/300px-Apotheosis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Apotheosis.jpg/400px-Apotheosis.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1506" data-file-height="958" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Apotheosis.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>The Apotheosis of War</i> (1871) by <a href="/wiki/Vasily_Vereshchagin" title="Vasily Vereshchagin">Vasily Vereshchagin</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Additionally, it has been estimated anywhere from 18% to 54% of Vietnam war veterans suffered from <a href="/wiki/Post_Traumatic_Stress_Disorder" class="mw-redirect" title="Post Traumatic Stress Disorder">Post Traumatic Stress Disorder</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1996_51-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1996-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Based on 1860 census figures, 8% of all white American males aged 13 to 43 died in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>, including about 6% in the North and approximately 18% in the South.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The war remains the deadliest conflict in American history, resulting in the deaths of 620,000 military personnel. <a href="/wiki/United_States_military_casualties_of_war" title="United States military casualties of war">United States military casualties of war</a> since 1775 have totaled over two million. Of the 60 million European military personnel who were mobilized in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, 8 million were killed, 7 million were permanently disabled, and 15 million were seriously injured.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg/220px-DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg/330px-DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg/440px-DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="403" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DeadCrowIndians1874.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The remains of dead <a href="/wiki/Crow_Indians" class="mw-redirect" title="Crow Indians">Crow Indians</a> killed and scalped by Sioux c. 1874</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a>'s retreat from Moscow, more French military personnel died of <a href="/wiki/Typhus" title="Typhus">typhus</a> than were killed by the Russians.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Of the 450,000 soldiers who crossed the <a href="/wiki/Neman_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Neman River">Neman</a> on 25 June 1812, less than 40,000 returned. More military personnel were killed from 1500–1914 by typhus than from military action.<sup id="cite_ref-TIME_Magazine_1940_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TIME_Magazine_1940-55">[55]</a></sup> In addition, if it were not for modern medical advances there would be thousands more dead from disease and infection. For instance, during the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years' War">Seven Years' War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> reported it conscripted 184,899 sailors, of whom 133,708 died of disease or were 'missing'.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is estimated that between 1985 and 1994, 378,000 people per year died due to war.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_civilians">On civilians</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Civilian_casualties" title="Civilian casualties">Civilian casualties</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Hanging_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/The_Hanging_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg/220px-The_Hanging_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg" width="220" height="97" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/The_Hanging_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg/330px-The_Hanging_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/The_Hanging_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg/440px-The_Hanging_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1458" data-file-height="643" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Hanging_by_Jacques_Callot.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/Les_Grandes_Mis%C3%A8res_de_la_guerre" title="Les Grandes Misères de la guerre">Les Grandes Misères de la guerre</a></i> depict the destruction unleashed on civilians during the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Most wars have resulted in significant loss of life, along with destruction of infrastructure and resources (which may lead to <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>, disease, and death in the <a href="/wiki/Civilian" title="Civilian">civilian</a> <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">population</a>). During the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years' War">Thirty Years' War</a> in Europe, the population of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> was reduced by 15 to 40 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> Civilians in war zones may also be subject to war atrocities such as <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a>, while survivors may suffer the psychological aftereffects of witnessing the destruction of war.</p>
<p>Most estimates of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_casualties" title="World War II casualties">World War II casualties</a> indicate around 60 million people died, 40 million of which were civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Deaths in the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> were around <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_casualties_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="World War II casualties of the Soviet Union">27 million</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Since a high proportion of those killed were young men who had not yet fathered any children, population growth in the postwar Soviet Union was much lower than it otherwise would have been.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_economy">On the economy</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Military_Keynesianism" title="Military Keynesianism">Military Keynesianism</a></div>
<p>Once a war has ended, losing nations are sometimes required to pay <a href="/wiki/War_reparations" title="War reparations">war reparations</a> to the victorious nations. In certain cases, land is ceded to the victorious nations. For example, the territory of <a href="/wiki/Alsace-Lorraine" title="Alsace-Lorraine">Alsace-Lorraine</a> has been traded between France and Germany on three different occasions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Typically, war becomes intertwined with the economy and many wars are partially or entirely based on economic reasons. Some economists<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> believe war can stimulate a country's economy (high government spending for World War II is often credited with bringing the USA out of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> by most <a href="/wiki/Keynesian" class="mw-redirect" title="Keynesian">Keynesian</a> economists) but in many cases, such as the wars of Louis XIV, the <a href="/wiki/Franco-Prussian_War" title="Franco-Prussian War">Franco-Prussian War</a>, and <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, warfare primarily results in damage the economy of the countries involved. For example, Russia's involvement in World War I took such a toll on the Russian economy that it almost collapsed and greatly contributed to the start of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_of_1917" class="mw-redirect" title="Russian Revolution of 1917">Russian Revolution of 1917</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II">World War II</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cyprian_War.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cyprian_War.jpg/170px-Cyprian_War.jpg" width="170" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cyprian_War.jpg/255px-Cyprian_War.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Cyprian_War.jpg/340px-Cyprian_War.jpg 2x" data-file-width="589" data-file-height="768" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cyprian_War.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ruins of Warsaw's Napoleon Square in the aftermath of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> was the most financially costly conflict in history: its belligerents cumulatively spent about a trillion U.S. dollars on the <a href="/wiki/War_effort" title="War effort">war effort</a> (as adjusted to 1940 prices).<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> of the 1930s ended as nations increased their production of war materials.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the end of the war, 70% of European industrial infrastructure was destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Property damage in the Soviet Union inflicted by the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Front_(World_War_II)" title="Eastern Front (World War II)">Axis invasion</a> was estimated at a value of 679 billion rubles. The combined damage consisted of complete or partial destruction of 1,710 cities and towns, 70,000 villages/hamlets, 2,508 church buildings, 31,850 industrial establishments, 40,000&#160;mi (64,374&#160;km) of railroad, 4100 railroad stations, 40,000 hospitals, 84,000 schools, and 43,000 public libraries.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="On_the_arts">On the arts</span></h3>
<p>War leads to <a href="/wiki/Forced_migration" class="mw-redirect" title="Forced migration">forced migration</a> causing potentially large displacements of population. Among forced migrants there are usually relatively large shares of artists and other types of creative people, causing so the war effects to be particularly harmful for the country’s creative potential in the long-run.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> War also has a negative effect on an artists’ individual life-cycle output.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>In war, cultural institutions, such as libraries, can become "targets in themselves; their elimination was a way to denigrate and demoralize the enemy population." The impact such destruction can have on a society is important because "in an era in which competing ideologies fuel internal and international conflict, the destruction of libraries and other items of cultural significance is neither random nor irrelevant. Preserving the world’s repositories of knowledge is crucial to ensuring that the darkest moments of history do not endlessly repeat themselves."<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Aims">Aims</span></h2>
<p>Entities deliberately contemplating going to war and entities considering whether to end a war may formulate <i>war aims</i> as an evaluation/propaganda tool. War aims may stand as a proxy for national-military resolve.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span></h3>
<p>Fried defines war aims as "the desired territorial, economic, military or other benefits expected following successful conclusion of a war".<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span></h3>
<p>Tangible/intangible aims:</p>
<ul>
<li>Tangible war aims may involve (for example) the acquisition of territory (as in the German goal of <a href="/wiki/Lebensraum" title="Lebensraum">Lebensraum</a> in the first half of the 20th century) or the recognition of economic concessions (as in the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Dutch_Wars" title="Anglo-Dutch Wars">Anglo-Dutch Wars</a>).</li>
<li>Intangible war aims – like the accumulation of credibility or reputation<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> – may have more tangible expression ("conquest restores prestige, annexation increases power").<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Explicit/implicit aims:</p>
<ul>
<li>Explicit war aims may involve published policy decisions.</li>
<li>Implicit war aims<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> can take the form of minutes of discussion, memoranda and instructions.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>Positive/negative aims:</p>
<ul>
<li>"Positive war aims" cover tangible outcomes.</li>
<li>"Negative war aims" forestall or prevent undesired outcomes.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>War aims can change in the course of conflict and may eventually morph into "peace conditions"<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> – the minimal conditions under which a state may cease to wage a particular war.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ongoing_conflicts">Ongoing conflicts</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_ongoing_military_conflicts" class="mw-redirect" title="List of ongoing military conflicts">List of ongoing military conflicts</a></div>
<p>There are currently dozens of ongoing armed conflicts around the world, the deadliest of which is the <a href="/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War" title="Syrian Civil War">Syrian Civil War</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Limiting_and_stopping">Limiting and stopping</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg/220px-Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg/330px-Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg/440px-Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Washington_March15_2003-02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Anti-war rally in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, March 15, 2003</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Anti-war_movement" title="Anti-war movement">Anti-war movement</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Aestheticization_of_violence" title="Aestheticization of violence">Aestheticization of violence</a></div>
<p>Religious groups have long formally opposed or sought to limit war as in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Vatican_Council" title="Second Vatican Council">Second Vatican Council</a> document <i>Gaudiem et Spes</i>: "Any act of war aimed indiscriminately at the destruction of entire cities of extensive areas along with their population is a crime against God and man himself. It merits unequivocal and unhesitating condemnation."<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>Anti-war movements have existed for every major war in the 20th century, including, most prominently, <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>. In the 21st century, worldwide anti-war movements occurred in response to the United States <a href="/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%9314)" class="mw-redirect" title="War in Afghanistan (2001–14)">invasion of Afghanistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq</a>. Protests <a href="/wiki/Opposition_to_the_War_in_Afghanistan_(2001%E2%80%93present)" class="mw-redirect" title="Opposition to the War in Afghanistan (2001–present)">opposing the War in Afghanistan</a> occurred in Europe, Asia, and the United States. Organizations like <a href="/wiki/Stop_the_War_Coalition" title="Stop the War Coalition">Stop the War Coalition</a>, based in the United Kingdom, worked on campaigning against the war.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mexican_Drug_War" title="Mexican Drug War">Mexican Drug War</a>, with estimated casualties of 40,000 since December 2006, has recently faced fundamental opposition.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> In 2011, the movement for peace and justice has started a popular middle-class movement against the war. It won the recognition of President Calderon, who began war.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Theories_for_motivation">Theories for motivation</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Szigetvar_1566.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Szigetvar_1566.jpg/170px-Szigetvar_1566.jpg" width="170" height="243" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Szigetvar_1566.jpg/255px-Szigetvar_1566.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Szigetvar_1566.jpg/340px-Szigetvar_1566.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1272" data-file-height="1819" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Szigetvar_1566.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/List_of_campaigns_of_Suleiman_the_Magnificent" title="List of campaigns of Suleiman the Magnificent">Ottoman campaign</a> for territorial <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe" title="Ottoman wars in Europe">expansion in Europe</a> in 1566.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There is no scholarly agreement on which are the most common motivations for war.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carl_von_Clausewitz" title="Carl von Clausewitz">Carl von Clausewitz</a> said, 'Every age had its own kind of war, its own limiting conditions, and its own peculiar preconceptions.'<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychoanalytic_psychology">Psychoanalytic psychology</span></h3>
<p>Dutch psychoanalyst <a href="/wiki/Joost_Meerloo" title="Joost Meerloo">Joost Meerloo</a> held that, "War is often...a mass discharge of accumulated internal rage (where)...the inner fears of mankind are discharged in mass destruction."<sup id="cite_ref-meerloo_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meerloo-85">[85]</a></sup> Thus war can sometimes be a means by which man's own frustration at his inability to master his own self is expressed and temporarily relieved via his unleashing of destructive behavior upon <i>others.</i> In this destructive scenario, these <i>others</i> are made to serve as the scapegoat of unspoken and subconscious frustrations and fears.</p>
<p>Other psychoanalysts such as E.F.M. Durban and <a href="/wiki/John_Bowlby" title="John Bowlby">John Bowlby</a> have argued human beings are <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inherently</a> violent.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> This aggressiveness is fueled by <a href="/wiki/Displacement_(psychology)" title="Displacement (psychology)">displacement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Psychological_projection" title="Psychological projection">projection</a> where a person transfers his or her grievances into bias and hatred against other <a href="/wiki/Race_(human_classification)" class="mw-redirect" title="Race (human classification)">races</a>, religions, nations or <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideologies</a>. By this theory, the nation state preserves order in the local society while creating an outlet for aggression through warfare.</p>
<p>The Italian psychoanalyst Franco Fornari, a follower of <a href="/wiki/Melanie_Klein" title="Melanie Klein">Melanie Klein</a>, thought war was the paranoid or projective “elaboration” of mourning.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Fornari thought war and violence develop out of our “love need”: our wish to preserve and defend the sacred object to which we are attached, namely our early mother and our fusion with her. For the adult, nations are the sacred objects that generate warfare. Fornari focused upon sacrifice as the essence of war: the astonishing willingness of human beings to die for their country, to give over their bodies to their nation.</p>
<p>Despite Fornari's theory that man's altruistic desire for self-sacrifice for a noble cause is a contributing factor towards war,few wars have originated from a desire for war among the general populace.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Far more often the general population has been reluctantly drawn into war by its rulers. One psychological theory that looks at the leaders is advanced by Maurice Walsh.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> He argues the general populace is more neutral towards war and wars occur when leaders with a psychologically abnormal disregard for human life are placed into power. War is caused by leaders who seek war such as <a href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Hitler</a>. Such leaders most often come to power in times of crisis when the populace opts for a decisive leader, who then leads the nation to war.</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Naturally, the common people don't want war; neither in Russia nor in England nor in America, nor for that matter in Germany. That is understood. But, after all, it is the leaders of the country who determine the policy and it is always a simple matter to drag the people along, whether it is a democracy or a fascist dictatorship or a Parliament or a Communist dictatorship. ... the people can always be brought to the bidding of the leaders. That is easy. All you have to do is tell them they are being attacked and denounce the pacifists for lack of patriotism and exposing the country to danger. It works the same way in any country.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>— <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Göring">Hermann Göring</a> at the Nuremberg trials, April 18, 1946<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Evolutionary">Evolutionary</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_warfare" title="Prehistoric warfare">Prehistoric warfare</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg/220px-Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg" width="220" height="268" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg/330px-Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg/440px-Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1500" data-file-height="1827" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aftermath_Battle_of_Triboltingen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Women and priests retrieve the dead bodies of Swabian soldiers just outside the city gates of Constance after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Schwaderloh" title="Battle of Schwaderloh">battle of Schwaderloh</a>. <i>(<a href="/wiki/Luzerner_Schilling" title="Luzerner Schilling">Luzerner Schilling</a>)</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Several theories concern the evolutionary origins of warfare. There are two main schools: One sees organized warfare as emerging in or after the Mesolithic as a result of complex social organization and greater population density and <a href="/wiki/Competition" title="Competition">competition</a> over resources; the other sees human warfare as a more ancient practice derived from common animal tendencies, such as territoriality and sexual competition.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>The latter school argues that since warlike behavior patterns are found in many primate species such as <a href="/wiki/Chimpanzee" title="Chimpanzee">chimpanzees</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ApesWar_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ApesWar-92">[92]</a></sup> as well as in many <a href="/wiki/Ant" title="Ant">ant</a> species,<sup id="cite_ref-AntsLandmines_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AntsLandmines-93">[93]</a></sup> group conflict may be a general feature of animal social behavior. Some proponents of the idea argue that war, while innate, has been intensified greatly by developments of technology and social organization such as weaponry and states.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Psychologist and linguist <a href="/wiki/Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker">Steven Pinker</a> argued that war-related behaviors may have been naturally selected in the ancestral environment due to the benefits of victory.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (July 2016)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> He also argued that in order to have credible <a href="/wiki/Deterrence_theory" title="Deterrence theory">deterrence</a> against other groups (as well as on an individual level), it was important to have a reputation for retaliation, causing humans to develop instincts for <a href="/wiki/Revenge" title="Revenge">revenge</a> as well as for protecting a group's (or an individual's) reputation ("<a href="/wiki/Honor" class="mw-redirect" title="Honor">honor</a>").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_(panorama).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG/260px-Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG" width="260" height="126" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG/390px-Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG/520px-Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_%28panorama%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="8763" data-file-height="4240" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Reproduction_of_Bonampak_murals_(panorama).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Increasing population and constant warfare among the <a href="/wiki/Maya_warfare" title="Maya warfare">Maya</a> city-states over resources may have contributed to the eventual <a href="/wiki/Classic_Maya_collapse" title="Classic Maya collapse">collapse</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Maya_civilization" title="Maya civilization">Maya civilization</a> by AD 900.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Crofoot and Wrangham have argued that warfare, if defined as group interactions in which "coalitions attempt to aggressively dominate or kill members of other groups", is a characteristic of most human societies. Those in which it has been lacking "tend to be societies that were politically dominated by their neighbors".<sup id="cite_ref-HumanPrimateAggression_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HumanPrimateAggression-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ashley_Montagu" title="Ashley Montagu">Ashley Montagu</a> strongly denied universalistic instinctual arguments, arguing that social factors and childhood socialization are important in determining the nature and presence of warfare. Thus, he argues, warfare is not a universal human occurrence and appears to have been a historical invention, associated with certain types of human societies.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Montagu's argument is supported by ethnographic research conducted in societies where the concept of aggression seems to be entirely absent, e.g. the <a href="/wiki/Chewong" class="mw-redirect" title="Chewong">Chewong</a> and <a href="/wiki/Semai_people" title="Semai people">Semai</a> of the Malay peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> Bobbi S. Low has observed correlation between warfare and education, noting societies where warfare is commonplace encourage their children to be more aggressive.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic">Economic</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A,_T-55,_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg/220px-Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg/330px-Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg/440px-Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A%2C_T-55%2C_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2860" data-file-height="1890" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Disabled_Iraqi_T-54A,_T-55,_Type_59_or_Type_69_tank_and_burning_Kuwaiti_oil_field.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Kuwaiti <a href="/wiki/Oil_well" title="Oil well">oil wells</a> on fire, during the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_War" title="Gulf War">Gulf War</a>, 1 March 1991</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>War can be seen as a growth of economic competition in a competitive international system. In this view wars begin as a pursuit of markets for <a href="/wiki/Natural_resource" title="Natural resource">natural resources</a> and for wealth. War has also been linked to <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a> by economic historians and development economists studying <a href="/wiki/State-building" title="State-building">state-building</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fiscal_capacity" title="Fiscal capacity">fiscal capacity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> While this theory has been applied to many conflicts, such counter arguments become less valid as the increasing mobility of capital and information level the distributions of wealth worldwide, or when considering that it is relative, not absolute, wealth differences that may fuel wars. There are those on the extreme <a href="/wiki/Right_(politics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Right (politics)">right</a> of the political spectrum who provide support, fascists in particular, by asserting a natural right of a strong nation to whatever the weak cannot hold by force.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Some centrist, capitalist, world leaders, including <a href="/wiki/Presidents_of_the_United_States" class="mw-redirect" title="Presidents of the United States">Presidents of the United States</a> and U.S. <a href="/wiki/General_officer" title="General officer">Generals</a>, expressed support for an economic view of war.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marxist">Marxist</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> theory of war is quasi-economic in that it states all modern wars are caused by competition for resources and markets between great (<a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialist</a>) powers, claiming these wars are a natural result of the <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">class system</a>. Part of the theory is that war will disappear once a <a href="/wiki/World_revolution" title="World revolution">world revolution</a>, over-throwing free markets and class systems, has occurred. Marxist philosopher <a href="/wiki/Rosa_Luxemburg" title="Rosa Luxemburg">Rosa Luxemburg</a> theorized that <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialism</a> was the result of capitalist countries needing new <a href="/wiki/Market_(economics)" title="Market (economics)">markets</a>. Expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Means_of_production" title="Means of production">means of production</a> is only possible if there is a corresponding growth in <a href="/wiki/Consumer_demand" class="mw-redirect" title="Consumer demand">consumer demand</a>. Since the workers in a <a href="/wiki/Capitalist_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Capitalist economy">capitalist economy</a> would be unable to fill the demand, producers must expand into non-capitalist markets to find consumers for their goods, hence driving imperialism.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographic">Demographic</span></h3>
<p>Demographic theories can be grouped into two classes, Malthusian theories and <a href="/wiki/Youth_bulge" class="mw-redirect" title="Youth bulge">youth bulge</a> theories.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Malthusian">Malthusian</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG/220px-Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG/330px-Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG/440px-Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG 2x" data-file-width="1517" data-file-height="1006" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aerial_view_of_a_US_helicopter_as_it_flies_over_a_Mogadishu_residential_area.JPEG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. Marine helicopter on patrol in Somalia as part of the <a href="/wiki/Unified_Task_Force" title="Unified Task Force">Unified Task Force</a>, 1992</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Malthusian theories see expanding population and scarce resources as a source of violent conflict.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Urban_II" title="Pope Urban II">Pope Urban II</a> in 1095, on the eve of the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a>, spoke:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>For this land which you now inhabit, shut in on all sides by the sea and the mountain peaks, is too narrow for your large population; it scarcely furnishes food enough for its cultivators. Hence it is that you murder and devour one another, that you wage wars, and that many among you perish in civil strife. Let hatred, therefore, depart from among you; let your quarrels end. Enter upon the road to the Holy Sepulchre; wrest that land from a wicked race, and subject it to yourselves.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>This is one of the earliest expressions of what has come to be called the Malthusian theory of war, in which wars are caused by expanding populations and limited resources. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Malthus" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Malthus">Thomas Malthus</a> (1766–1834) wrote that populations always increase until they are limited by war, disease, or <a href="/wiki/Famine" title="Famine">famine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Youth_bulge">Youth bulge</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Median_age.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Median_age.png/310px-Median_age.png" width="310" height="136" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Median_age.png/465px-Median_age.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Median_age.png/620px-Median_age.png 2x" data-file-width="1425" data-file-height="625" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Median_age.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Population_pyramid" title="Population pyramid">Median age</a> by country. A youth bulge is evident for <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Africa" title="Demographics of Africa">Africa</a>, and to a lesser extent for South and Southeast Asia and Central America.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to Heinsohn, who proposed <a href="/wiki/Youth_bulge" class="mw-redirect" title="Youth bulge">youth bulge</a> theory in its most generalized form, a youth bulge occurs when 30 to 40 percent of the males of a nation belong to the "fighting age" cohorts from 15 to 29 years of age. It will follow periods with <a href="/wiki/Total_fertility_rate" title="Total fertility rate">total fertility rates</a> as high as 4–8 children per woman with a 15–29-year delay.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Heinsohn saw both past "Christianist" European colonialism and imperialism, as well as today's Islamist civil unrest and terrorism as results of high birth rates producing youth bulges.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Among prominent historical events that have been attributed to youth bulges are the role played by the historically large youth cohorts in the rebellion and revolution waves of early modern Europe, including the <a href="/wiki/French_Revolution" title="French Revolution">French Revolution</a> of 1789,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> and the effect of economic depression upon the largest German youth cohorts ever in explaining the rise of <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a> in Germany in the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> The 1994 <a href="/wiki/Rwandan_Genocide" class="mw-redirect" title="Rwandan Genocide">Rwandan Genocide</a> has also been analyzed as following a massive youth bulge.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>Youth bulge theory has been subjected to statistical analysis by the World Bank,<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Population_Action_International" title="Population Action International">Population Action International</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Institute_for_Population_and_Development" title="Berlin Institute for Population and Development">Berlin Institute for Population and Development</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Youth bulge theories have been criticized as leading to racial, gender and age discrimination.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rationalist">Rationalist</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg/220px-LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg" width="220" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg/330px-LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg/440px-LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="914" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:LRPs_directing_artillery.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. soldiers directing artillery on enemy trucks in A Shau Valley, April 1968.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Rationalism_(international_relations)" title="Rationalism (international relations)">Rationalism</a> is an <a href="/wiki/International_relations_theory" title="International relations theory">international relations theory</a> or framework. Rationalism (and <a href="/wiki/Neorealism_(international_relations)" title="Neorealism (international relations)">Neorealism (international relations)</a>) operate under the assumption that states or international actors are rational, seek the best possible outcomes for themselves, and desire to avoid the costs of war.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-116">[116]</a></sup> Under a <a href="/wiki/Game_theory" title="Game theory">Game theory</a> approach, rationalist theories posit all actors can bargain, would be better off if war did not occur, and likewise seek to understand why war nonetheless reoccurs. In "Rationalist Explanations for War", <a href="/wiki/James_Fearon" title="James Fearon">James Fearon</a> examined three rationalist explanations for why some countries engage in war:</p>
<ol>
<li>Issue Indivisibilities</li>
<li>Incentives to Misrepresent or <a href="/wiki/Information_asymmetry" title="Information asymmetry">Information Asymmetry</a></li>
<li>Commitment Problems<sup id="cite_ref-:0_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-116">[116]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>"Issue indivisibility" occurs when the two parties cannot avoid war by bargaining, because the thing over which they are fighting cannot be shared between them, but only owned entirely by one side or the other.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_(Vietnam_War)_001.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg/220px-U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg" width="220" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg/330px-U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg/440px-U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_%28Vietnam_War%29_001.jpg 2x" data-file-width="673" data-file-height="518" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:U.S._Marines_in_Operation_Allen_Brook_(Vietnam_War)_001.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
U.S. Marines direct a concentration of fire at the enemy, Vietnam, 8 May 1968</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Information_asymmetry" title="Information asymmetry">Information asymmetry</a> with incentives to misrepresent" occurs when two countries have secrets about their individual capabilities, and do not agree on either: who would win a war between them, or the magnitude of state's victory or loss. For instance, <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Blainey" title="Geoffrey Blainey">Geoffrey Blainey</a> argues that war is a result of miscalculation of strength. He cites historical examples of war and demonstrates, "war is usually the outcome of a diplomatic crisis which cannot be solved because both sides have conflicting estimates of their bargaining power."<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Thirdly, bargaining may fail due to the states' inability to make credible commitments.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within the rationalist tradition, some theorists have suggested individuals engaged in war suffer a normal level of <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_bias" title="Cognitive bias">cognitive bias</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> but are still "as rational as you and me".<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> According to philosopher <a href="/wiki/Iain_King" title="Iain King">Iain King</a>, "Most instigators of conflict overrate their chances of success, while most participants underrate their chances of injury...."<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> King asserts that "Most catastrophic military decisions are rooted in <a href="/wiki/GroupThink" class="mw-redirect" title="GroupThink">GroupThink</a>" which is faulty, but still rational.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rationalist theory focused around bargaining is currently under debate. The Iraq War proved to be an anomaly that undercuts the validity of applying rationalist theory to some wars.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_science">Political science</span></h3>
<p>The statistical analysis of war was pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Fry_Richardson" title="Lewis Fry Richardson">Lewis Fry Richardson</a> following <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. More recent databases of wars and armed conflict have been assembled by the <a href="/wiki/Correlates_of_War" title="Correlates of War">Correlates of War</a> Project, Peter Brecke and the <a href="/wiki/Uppsala_Conflict_Data_Program" title="Uppsala Conflict Data Program">Uppsala Conflict Data Program</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The following subsections consider causes of war from system, societal, and individual levels of analysis. This kind of division was first proposed by <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Waltz" title="Kenneth Waltz">Kenneth Waltz</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Man,_the_State,_and_War" title="Man, the State, and War">Man, the State, and War</a></i> and has been often used by political scientists since then.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:143</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="System-level_theories">System-level theories</span></h4>
<p>There are several different <a href="/wiki/International_relations_theory" title="International relations theory">international relations theory</a> schools. Supporters of <a href="/wiki/Realism_in_international_relations" class="mw-redirect" title="Realism in international relations">realism in international relations</a> argue that the motivation of states is the quest for security, and conflicts can arise from the inability to distinguish defense from offense, which is called the <a href="/wiki/Security_dilemma" title="Security dilemma">security dilemma</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:145</sup></p>
<p>Within the realist school as represented by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hans_Morgenthau" title="Hans Morgenthau">Hans Morgenthau</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Neorealism_(international_relations)" title="Neorealism (international relations)">neorealist</a> school represented by scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Waltz" title="Kenneth Waltz">Kenneth Waltz</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Mearsheimer" title="John Mearsheimer">John Mearsheimer</a>, two main sub-theories are:</p>
<ol>
<li><a href="/wiki/Balance_of_power_(international_relations)" title="Balance of power (international relations)">Balance of power</a> theory: States have the goal of preventing a single state from becoming a hegemon, and war is the result of the would-be hegemon's persistent attempts at power acquisition. In this view, an international system with more equal distribution of power is more stable, and "movements toward unipolarity are destabilizing."<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:147</sup> However, evidence has shown power <a href="/wiki/Polarity_(international_relations)" title="Polarity (international relations)">polarity</a> is not actually a major factor in the occurrence of wars.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:147–48</sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Power_transition_theory" title="Power transition theory">Power transition theory</a>: Hegemons impose stabilizing conditions on the world order, but they eventually decline, and war occurs when a declining hegemon is challenged by another rising power or aims to preemptively suppress them.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:148</sup> On this view, unlike for balance-of-power theory, wars become <i>more</i> probable when power is more equally distributed. This "power preponderance" hypothesis has empirical support.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:148</sup></li>
</ol>
<p>The two theories are not mutually exclusive, and may be used to explain disparate events according to the circumstance.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:148</sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Liberalism_(international_relations)" title="Liberalism (international relations)">Liberalism</a> at it relates to international relations emphasizes factors such as trade, and its role in disincentivizing conflict which will damage economic relations. Realists<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> respond that military force may sometimes be at least as effective as trade at achieving economic benefits, especially historically if not as much today.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:149</sup> Furthermore, trade relations which result in a high level of dependency, may escalate tensions and lead to conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:150</sup> Empirical data on the relationship of trade to peace are mixed, and moreover, some evidence suggests countries at war don't necessarily trade less with each other.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:150</sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Societal-level_theories">Societal-level theories</span></h4>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Diversionary_foreign_policy" title="Diversionary foreign policy">Diversionary theory</a>, also known as the "scapegoat hypothesis", suggests the politically powerful may use war to as a diversion or to rally domestic popular support.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:152</sup> This is supported by literature showing outgroup hostility <a href="/wiki/Realistic_conflict_theory" title="Realistic conflict theory">enhances</a> ingroup <a href="/wiki/Group_cohesiveness" title="Group cohesiveness">bonding</a>, and a significant domestic "rally effect" has been demonstrated when conflicts begin.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:152–13</sup> However, studies examining the increased use of force as a function of need for internal political support are more mixed.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:152–53</sup> U.S. war-time presidential popularity surveys taken during the presidencies of several recent U.S. leaders have supported diversionary theory.<sup id="cite_ref-MilitaryAdventurism_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MilitaryAdventurism-125">[125]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Individual-level_theories">Individual-level theories</span></h4>
<p>These theories suggest differences in people's personalities, decision-making, emotions, belief systems, and biases are important in determining whether conflicts get out of hand.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:157</sup> For instance, it has been proposed that conflict is modulated by <a href="/wiki/Bounded_rationality" title="Bounded rationality">bounded rationality</a> and various <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_biases" class="mw-redirect" title="Cognitive biases">cognitive biases</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1998_124-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1998-124">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:157</sup> such as <a href="/wiki/Prospect_theory" title="Prospect theory">prospect theory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Levy1997_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levy1997-126">[126]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg/220px-Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg/330px-Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg/440px-Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Morgen_nach_der_Schlacht967b.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>Morning after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Waterloo" title="Battle of Waterloo">Battle of Waterloo</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/John_Heaviside_Clark" title="John Heaviside Clark">John Heaviside Clark</a>, 1816</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">morality</a> of war has been the subject of debate for thousands of years.<sup id="cite_ref-DeForrest_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeForrest-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>The two principle aspects of ethics in war, according the <a href="/wiki/Just_War_Theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Just War Theory">Just War Theory</a> are <a href="/wiki/Jus_ad_bellum" title="Jus ad bellum">jus ad bellum</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Jus_in_bello" class="mw-redirect" title="Jus in bello">Jus in bello</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Codevilla.2C_Seabury_1989_304_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Codevilla.2C_Seabury_1989_304-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Jus ad bellum</i> (right to war), dictates which unfriendly acts and circumstances justify a proper authority in declaring war on another nation. There are six main criteria for the declaration of a just war: first, any just war must be declared by a lawful authority; second, it must be a just and righteous cause, with sufficient gravity to merit large-scale violence; third, the just belligerent must have rightful intentions – namely, that they seek to advance good and curtail evil; fourth, a just belligerent must have a reasonable chance of success; fifth, the war must be a last resort; and sixth, the ends being sought must be proportional to means being used.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Jus_in_bello" class="mw-redirect" title="Jus in bello">Jus in bello</a></i> (right in war), is the set of ethical rules when conducting war. The two main principles are proportionality and discrimination. Proportionality regards how much force is necessary and morally appropriate to the ends being sought and the injustice suffered.<sup id="cite_ref-Moseley_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moseley-132">[132]</a></sup> The principle of discrimination determines who are the legitimate targets in a war, and specifically makes a separation between combatants, who it is permissible to kill, and who it is not.<sup id="cite_ref-Moseley_132-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moseley-132">[132]</a></sup> Failure to follow these rules can result in the loss of legitimacy for the just-war-belligerent.<sup id="cite_ref-Codevilla.2C_Seabury_1989_304_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Codevilla.2C_Seabury_1989_304-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg/220px-RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg/330px-RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg/440px-RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="426" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RIAN_archive_324_In_besieged_Leningrad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
In besieged <a href="/wiki/Leningrad" class="mw-redirect" title="Leningrad">Leningrad</a>. "Hitler ordered that Moscow and Leningrad were to be razed to the ground; their inhabitants were to be annihilated or driven out by starvation. These intentions were part of the '<a href="/wiki/General_Plan_East" class="mw-redirect" title="General Plan East">General Plan East</a>'." – <i>The Oxford Companion to World War II.</i><sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Just War Theory was foundational in the creation of the United Nations and in <a href="/wiki/International_Law" class="mw-redirect" title="International Law">International Law</a>'s regulations on legitimate war.<sup id="cite_ref-DeForrest_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeForrest-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>Fascism, and the ideals it encompasses, such as <a href="/wiki/Pragmatism" title="Pragmatism">Pragmatism</a>, racism, and <a href="/wiki/Social_Darwinism" title="Social Darwinism">social Darwinism</a>, hold that violence is good.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Pragmatism holds that war and violence can be good if it serves the ends of the people, without regard for universal morality. Racism holds that violence is good so that a master race can be established, or to purge an inferior race from the earth, or both. Social Darwinism asserts that violence is sometimes necessary to weed the unfit from society so civilization can flourish. These are broad <a href="/wiki/Archetypes" class="mw-redirect" title="Archetypes">archetypes</a> for the general position that <a href="/wiki/The_ends_justify_the_means" class="mw-redirect" title="The ends justify the means">the ends justify the means</a>. Lewis Coser, U.S. conflict theorist and sociologist, argued conflict provides a function and a process whereby a succession of new equilibriums are created. Thus, the struggle of opposing forces, rather than being disruptive, may be a means of balancing and maintaining a social structure or society.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup></p>
